<a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a></div></th></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Official name</th><td class="infobox-data">Silk Roads: the Routes Network of Chang'an-Tianshan</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Type</th><td class="infobox-data">Cultural</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Criteria</th><td class="infobox-data">ii, iii, iv, vi</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Designated</th><td class="infobox-data">2014 <small>(38th <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Committee" title="World Heritage Committee">session</a>)</small></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Reference no.</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="external text" href="https://whc.unesco.org/en/list/1442" rel="nofollow">1442</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Region</th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_World_Heritage_Sites_in_Asia" title="List of World Heritage Sites in Asia">Asia-Pacific</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">
</th></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Silk Road</b> (<a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hant">絲綢之路</span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> was a network of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasian</a> <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">trade routes</a> active from the second century BCE until the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup> Spanning over 6,400 kilometers (4,000 miles), it played a central role in facilitating economic, cultural, political, and religious interactions between the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> First coined in the late 19th century, the name "Silk Road" has fallen into disuse among some modern historians in favor of <b>Silk Routes</b>, which more accurately describes the intricate web of land and sea routes connecting <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Europe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Silk Road derives its name from the highly lucrative trade of <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk textiles</a> that were <a href="/wiki/Silk_industry_in_China" title="Silk industry in China">produced almost exclusively</a> in China. The network began with the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty's</a> expansion into <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> around 114 BCE, <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">which largely pacified</a> the once untamed region. Imperial envoy <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> was commissioned to explore the unknown lands beyond the region in search of potential trading partners and allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boulnois_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-boulnois-6">[6]</a></sup> The information and goods gathered by these expeditions piqued Chinese interest and prompted formal diplomatic and commercial dispatches, as well as efforts to protect the routes with soldiers and an extension of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Wall_of_China" title="Great Wall of China">Great Wall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, which stretched from eastern Anatolia to Afghanistan, provided a bridge to East Africa and the Mediterranean, particularly the nascent Roman Empire. By the early first century CE, Chinese silk was widely sought-after in Rome, Egypt, and Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-:5-2">[2]</a></sup> Other lucrative commodities from the East included tea, dyes, perfumes, and porcelain; among Western exports were horses, camels, honey, wine, and gold. Aside from generating substantial wealth for emerging mercantile classes, the proliferation of goods such as <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a> greatly altered the trajectory of various realms, if not world history. 
</p><p>During its roughly 1,500 years of existence, the Silk Road endured the rise and fall of numerous empires and several major calamities including the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_and_conquests" title="Mongol invasions and conquests">Mongol conquests</a>. After these calamities, the Silk Road often emerged stronger than before, including after the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and its offshoot the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a>. As a highly decentralized network, security was sparse. Travelers faced constant threats of banditry and nomadic raiders, and long expanses of inhospitable terrain. Few individuals crossed the entirety of the Silk Road, instead relying on a succession of middlemen based at various stopping points along the way.
</p><p>The Silk Road trade played a significant role in opening political and economic relations between China, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Korea#Proto–Three_Kingdoms" title="History of Korea">Korea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_Japan#Yayoi_period" title="History of Japan">Japan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-4">[4]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_South_Asian_history" title="Outline of South Asian history">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire#Relations_with_China" title="Sasanian Empire">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Europe#Early_antiquity_period" title="History of Europe">Europe</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa#Ancient_history" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Romans_in_Arabia" title="History of the Romans in Arabia">Arabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bentley1993p32_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bentley1993p32-9">[9]</a></sup> In addition to goods, the network facilitated an unprecedented exchange of ideas, religions (<a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">especially Buddhism</a>), philosophies, and scientific discoveries, many of which were <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretised</a> or reshaped by the societies that encountered them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Likewise, a wide variety of people utilized the routes, including migrants, refugees, missionaries, artisans, diplomats, and soldiers. Diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Plague_(disease)" title="Plague (disease)">plague</a> also spread along the Silk Road, possibly contributing to the Black Death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Guardian_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Guardian-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>Despite repeatedly surviving many geopolitical changes and disruptions, the Silk Road abruptly ended with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1453, which almost immediately severed trade between East and West. This prompted European efforts to seek alternative routes to Eastern riches, thereby ushering the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_colonialism" title="European colonialism">European colonialism</a>, and a more intensified process of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>, which had arguably begun with the Silk Road. The network's influence survives into the 21st century. One of the world's best known historical figures, Marco Polo, was a Medieval Venetian merchant who was among the earliest Westerners <a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">to visit and describe the East</a>. The name "New Silk Road" is used to describe several large <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a> projects seeking to expand transportation through many of the historic trade routes; among the best known include the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Land_Bridge" title="Eurasian Land Bridge">Eurasian Land Bridge</a> and the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Belt_and_Road_Initiative" title="Belt and Road Initiative">Belt and Road Initiative</a> (BRI). In June 2014, <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a> designated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silk_Road_UNESCO_World_Heritage_Sites" title="Silk Road UNESCO World Heritage Sites">Chang'an-Tianshan corridor of the Silk Road</a> as a <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_sites_in_India" title="Silk Road sites in India">Indian portion</a> remains on the tentative site list.
</p>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Woven_silk,_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="566" data-file-width="1116" decoding="async" height="112" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg/220px-Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg/330px-Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg/440px-Woven_silk%2C_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Woven_silk,_Western_Han_Dynasty.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Woven <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> textile from Tomb No. 1 at <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> province, China, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">dated to the Western Han Era</a>, 2nd century BCE</div></div></div>
<p>The Silk Road derives its name from the lucrative trade in <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_silk" title="History of silk">first developed in China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13">[13]</a></sup> and a major reason for the connection of trade routes into an extensive transcontinental network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waugh_2007,_p._4_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waugh_2007,_p._4-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Silk_Roads_1998_pp._1-2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Silk_Roads_1998_pp._1-2-15">[15]</a></sup> It derives from the German term <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Seidenstraße</i></span> (literally "Silk Road") and was first popularized in 1877 by <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Richthofen" title="Ferdinand von Richthofen">Ferdinand von Richthofen</a>, who made seven expeditions to China from 1868 to 1872.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Silk_Roads_1998_pp._1-2_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Silk_Roads_1998_pp._1-2-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p156_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p156-18">[18]</a></sup> However, the term itself has been in use in decades prior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> The alternative translation "Silk Route" is also used occasionally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Although the term was coined in the 19th century, it did not gain widespread acceptance in academia or popularity among the public until the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p156_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p156-18">[18]</a></sup> The first book entitled <i>The Silk Road</i> was by Swedish geographer <a href="/wiki/Sven_Hedin" title="Sven Hedin">Sven Hedin</a> in 1938.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p156_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p156-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The use of the term 'Silk Road' is not without its detractors. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a> contends that the maritime <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">spice trade</a> with <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">India and Arabia</a> was far more consequential for <a href="/wiki/Roman_economy" title="Roman economy">the economy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">silk trade with China</a>, which at sea was conducted mostly through India and on land was handled by numerous intermediaries such as the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Going as far as to call the whole thing a "myth" of modern academia, Ball argues that there was no coherent overland trade system and no free movement of goods <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">from East Asia to the West</a> until the period of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> He notes that traditional authors discussing east–west trade such as <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a> never labelled any route a "silk" one in particular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p156_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p156-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The southern stretches of the Silk Road, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>) to Eastern China, were first used for <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> and not silk, as long as 5000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BCE" title="BCE">BCE</a>, and is still in use for this purpose. The term "Jade Road" would have been more appropriate than "Silk Road" had it not been for the far larger and geographically wider nature of the silk trade; the term is in current use in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood2004_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood2004-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursors">Precursors</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Chinese_and_Central_Asian_contacts_.282nd_millennium_BCE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chinese_and_Central_Asian_contacts_(2nd_millennium_BCE)">Chinese and Central Asian contacts (2nd millennium BCE)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChineseJadePlaques.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="953" data-file-width="978" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/220px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/330px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/ChineseJadePlaques.JPG/440px-ChineseJadePlaques.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChineseJadePlaques.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steatite" title="Steatite">steatite</a> plaques, in the <a href="/wiki/Scythian_art" title="Scythian art">Scythian-style</a> <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal art</a> of the steppes. 4th–3rd century BCE. <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Inner_Asia" title="Inner Asia">Central Eurasia</a> has been known from ancient times for its horse riding and horse breeding communities, and the overland <a href="/wiki/Steppe_Route" title="Steppe Route">Steppe Route</a> across the northern steppes of Central Eurasia was in use long before that of the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13">[13]</a></sup> Archeological sites such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berel_burial_ground" title="Berel burial ground">Berel burial ground</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, confirmed that the nomadic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arimaspians" title="Arimaspians">Arimaspians</a> were not only breeding horses for trade but also produced great craftsmen able to propagate exquisite art pieces along the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> From the 2nd millennium BCE, <a href="/wiki/Nephrite" title="Nephrite">nephrite</a> jade was being traded from mines in the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yarkent_County" title="Yarkent County">Yarkand</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> to China. Significantly, these mines were not very far from the <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spinel" title="Spinel">spinel</a> ("Balas Ruby") mines in <a href="/wiki/Badakhshan" title="Badakhshan">Badakhshan</a>, and, although separated by the formidable <a href="/wiki/Pamir_Mountains" title="Pamir Mountains">Pamir Mountains</a>, routes across them were apparently in use from very early times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tarim_mummies" title="Tarim mummies">Tarim mummies</a>, mummies of non-Mongoloid, apparently Caucasoid, individuals, have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, in the area of <a href="/wiki/Loulan_Kingdom" title="Loulan Kingdom">Loulan</a> located along the Silk Road 200 kilometres (124 miles) east of Yingpan, dating to as early as 1600 BCE and suggesting very ancient contacts between East and West. These mummified remains may have been of people who spoke <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>, which remained in use in the Tarim Basin, in the modern day <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> region, until replaced by Turkic influences from the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> culture to the north and by Chinese influences from the eastern <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>, who spoke a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sino-Tibetan_language" title="Sino-Tibetan language">Sino-Tibetan language</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some remnants of what was probably Chinese silk dating from 1070 BCE have been found in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>. The Great Oasis cities of Central Asia played a crucial role in the effective functioning of the Silk Road trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The originating source seems sufficiently reliable, but silk degrades very rapidly, so it cannot be verified whether it was cultivated silk (which almost certainly came from China) or a type of <i><a href="/wiki/Wild_silk" title="Wild silk">wild silk</a></i>, which might have come from the Mediterranean or Middle East.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Following contacts between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_regions_of_China" title="Metropolitan regions of China">Metropolitan China</a> and nomadic western border territories in the 8th century BCE, gold was introduced from Central Asia, and Chinese jade carvers began to make imitation designs of the steppes, adopting the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scythian" title="Scythian">Scythian</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">animal art</a> of the steppes (depictions of animals locked in combat). This style is particularly reflected in the rectangular belt plaques made of gold and bronze, with other versions in jade and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steatite" title="Steatite">steatite</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> An elite burial near <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>, Germany, dated to the 6th century BCE, was excavated and found to have not only <a href="/wiki/Bronze_sculpture" title="Bronze sculpture">Greek bronzes</a> but also Chinese silks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christopoulos_2012_footnote56_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-christopoulos_2012_footnote56-28">[28]</a></sup> Similar animal-shaped pieces of art and wrestler motifs on belts have been found in <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythian</a> grave sites stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> region all the way to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warring_States" title="Warring States">Warring States</a> era archaeological sites in <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a> (at Aluchaideng) and <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a> (at <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Keshengzhuang&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Keshengzhuang (page does not exist)">Keshengzhuang</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Keshengzhuang" title="de:Keshengzhuang">de</a>]</span>) in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christopoulos_2012_footnote56_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-christopoulos_2012_footnote56-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The expansion of Scythian cultures, stretching from the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Hungarian plain</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_Mountains" title="Carpathian Mountains">Carpathian Mountains</a> to the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kansu" title="Kansu">Kansu</a> Corridor, and linking the Middle East with Northern India and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, undoubtedly played an important role in the development of the Silk Road. Scythians accompanied the <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Esarhaddon" title="Esarhaddon">Esarhaddon</a> on his invasion of Egypt, and their distinctive triangular arrowheads have been found as far south as <a href="/wiki/Aswan" title="Aswan">Aswan</a>. These nomadic peoples were dependent upon neighbouring settled populations for a number of important technologies, and in addition to raiding vulnerable settlements for these commodities, they also encouraged long-distance merchants as a source of income through the enforced payment of tariffs. <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdians</a> played a major role in facilitating trade between China and Central Asia along the Silk Roads as late as the 10th century, their language serving as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for Asian trade as far back as the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Persian_Royal_Road_.28500.E2.80.93330_BCE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Persian_Royal_Road_(500–330_BCE)">Persian Royal Road (500–330 BCE)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2250" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/280px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/420px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg/560px-Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Empire_at_its_greatest_extent_according_to_Oxford_Atlas_of_World_History_2002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Persian_Empire" title="Achaemenid Persian Empire">Achaemenid Persian Empire</a> at its greatest extent, showing the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>By the time of <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> (c. 475 BCE), the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a> ran some 2,857 km (1,775 mi) from the city of <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Karun" title="Karun">Karun</a> (250 km (155 mi) east of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>) to the port of <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/%C4%B0zmir" title="İzmir">İzmir</a> in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>) on the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> It was maintained and protected by the Achaemenid Empire (c. 500–330 BCE) and had postal stations and relays at regular intervals. By having fresh horses and riders ready at each relay, royal couriers could carry messages and traverse the length of the road in nine days, while normal travelers took about three months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Expansion_of_the_Greek_Empire_.28329_BCE.E2.80.9310_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_the_Greek_Empire_(329_BCE–10_CE)">Expansion of the Greek Empire (329 BCE–10 CE)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="445" decoding="async" height="382" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/170px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/255px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/UrumqiWarrior.jpg/340px-UrumqiWarrior.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Soldier with a <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaur</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sampul_tapestry" title="Sampul tapestry">Sampul tapestry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> wool wall hanging, 3rd–2nd century BCE, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Museum" title="Xinjiang Museum">Xinjiang Museum</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urumqi" title="Urumqi">Urumqi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China.</div></div></div>
<p>The next major step toward the development of the Silk Road was the expansion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedonian_empire" title="Macedonian empire">Macedonian empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> into <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. In August 329 BCE, at the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a>, he founded the city of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Eschate" title="Alexandria Eschate">Alexandria Eschate</a> or "Alexandria The Furthest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Greeks remained in Central Asia for the next three centuries, first through the administration of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, and then with the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a> (250–125 BCE) in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, and Pakistan) and the later <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a> (180 BCE – 10 CE) in modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Pakistan" title="North Pakistan">Northern Pakistan</a> and Afghanistan. They continued to expand eastward, especially during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_I" title="Euthydemus I">Euthydemus</a> (230–200 BCE), who extended his control beyond Alexandria Eschate to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>. There are indications that he may have led expeditions as far as <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a> on the western edge of the <a href="/wiki/Taklamakan_Desert" title="Taklamakan Desert">Taklamakan Desert</a>, leading to the first known contacts between China and the West around 200 BCE.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> writes, "they extended their empire even as far as the <a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Seres</a> (China) and the Phryni."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Classical Greek philosophy</a> <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretised</a> with <a href="/wiki/Indian_philosophy" title="Indian philosophy">Indian philosophy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Initiation_in_China_.28130_BCE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiation_in_China_(130_BCE)">Initiation in China (130 BCE)</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">Protectorate of the Western Regions</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Heavenly_Horses" title="War of the Heavenly Horses">War of the Heavenly Horses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han–Xiongnu War">Han–Xiongnu War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">History of the Han dynasty</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a>, <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93India_relations" title="China–India relations">China–India relations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a></div>

.trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle .thumbcaption{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow>.thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/150px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/225px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg/300px-Silk_from_Mawangdui_2.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="778" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/150px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/225px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg/300px-Silk_from_Mawangdui.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Woven <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> textiles from Tomb No. 1 at <a href="/wiki/Mawangdui" title="Mawangdui">Mawangdui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Changsha" title="Changsha">Changsha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunan" title="Hunan">Hunan</a> province, China, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han_dynasty" title="Western Han dynasty">Western Han dynasty</a> period, dated 2nd century BCE</div></div></div></div>
<p>The Silk Road was initiated and spread by China's Han dynasty through exploration and <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_of_the_Western_Regions" title="Protectorate of the Western Regions">conquests in Central Asia</a>. With the Mediterranean linked to the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a>, the next step was to open a route across the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_Proper" title="China Proper">China Proper</a>. This extension came around 130 BCE, with the embassies of the Han dynasty to Central Asia following the reports of the ambassador <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> (who was originally sent to obtain an alliance with the <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>). Zhang Qian visited directly the kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dayuan" title="Dayuan">Dayuan</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferghana" title="Ferghana">Ferghana</a>, the territories of the Yuezhi in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a> country of <a href="/wiki/Daxia" title="Daxia">Daxia</a> with its remnants of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> rule, and <a href="/wiki/Kangju" title="Kangju">Kangju</a>. He also made reports on neighbouring countries that he did not visit, such as Anxi (<a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>), Tiaozhi (<a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>), Shendu (<a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Wusun" title="Wusun">Wusun</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Zhang Qian's report suggested the economic reason for Chinese expansion and wall-building westward, and trail-blazed the Silk Road, making it one of the most famous trade routes in history and in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>After winning the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Heavenly_Horses" title="War of the Heavenly Horses">War of the Heavenly Horses</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han–Xiongnu War">Han–Xiongnu War</a>, Chinese armies established themselves in Central Asia, initiating the Silk Route as a major avenue of international trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Li_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Li-40">[40]</a></sup> Some say that the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han_China" title="Emperor Wu of Han China">Emperor Wu</a> became interested in developing commercial relationships with the sophisticated urban civilizations of Ferghana, Bactria, and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>: "The Son of Heaven on hearing all this reasoned thus: Ferghana (Dayuan <i>"Great <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionians</a>"</i>) and the possessions of Bactria (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ta-Hsia" title="Ta-Hsia">Ta-Hsia</a>) and Parthian Empire (<a href="/wiki/Anxi_County" title="Anxi County">Anxi</a>) are large countries, full of rare things, with a population living in fixed abodes and given to occupations somewhat identical with those of the Chinese people, but with weak armies, and placing great value on the rich produce of China" (<i>Hou Hanshu</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Later_Han_History" title="Later Han History">Later Han History</a>). Others<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> say that Emperor Wu was mainly interested in <a href="/wiki/Han%E2%80%93Xiongnu_War" title="Han–Xiongnu War">fighting the Xiongnu</a> and that major trade began only after the Chinese pacified the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a>.
The Silk Roads' origin lay in the hands of the Chinese. The soil in China lacked <a href="/wiki/Selenium" title="Selenium">Selenium</a>, a deficiency which contributed to muscular weakness and reduced growth in horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Consequently, horses in China were too frail to support the weight of a Chinese soldier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The Chinese needed the superior horses that nomads bred on the Eurasian steppes, and nomads wanted things only agricultural societies produced, such as grain and silk. Even after the construction of the Great Wall, nomads gathered at the gates of the wall to exchange. Soldiers sent to guard the wall were often paid in silk which they traded with the nomads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Past its inception, the Chinese continued to dominate the Silk Roads, a process which was accelerated when "China snatched control of the Silk Road from the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Hsiung-nu</a>" and the Chinese general Cheng Ki "installed himself as protector of the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim</a> at Wu-lei, situated between <a href="/wiki/Karasahr" title="Karasahr">Kara Shahr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a>." "China's control of the Silk Road at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">later Han</a>, by ensuring the freedom of transcontinental trade along the double chain of oases north and south of the Tarim, favoured the dissemination of Buddhism in the river basin, and with it Indian literature and Hellenistic art."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HanHorse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="420" data-file-width="453" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HanHorse.jpg/220px-HanHorse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HanHorse.jpg/330px-HanHorse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/HanHorse.jpg/440px-HanHorse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HanHorse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A ceramic horse head and neck (broken from the body), from the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_dynasty" title="Eastern Han dynasty">Eastern Han dynasty</a> (1st–2nd century CE)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1162" data-file-width="1161" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/170px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/255px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg/340px-Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_coin_of_Contantius_II_337_361_found_in_Karghalik.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Bronze coin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Constantius_II" title="Constantius II">Constantius II</a> (337–361), found in <a href="/wiki/Kargilik_Town" title="Kargilik Town">Karghalik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></div></div></div><p>
The Chinese were also strongly attracted by the tall and powerful horses (named "<a href="/wiki/Ferghana_horse" title="Ferghana horse">Heavenly horses</a>") in the possession of the Dayuan (literally the "Great Ionians", the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greek kingdoms of Central Asia</a>), which were of capital importance in fighting the nomadic Xiongnu. They defeated the Dayuan in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han-Dayuan_war" title="Han-Dayuan war">Han-Dayuan war</a>. The Chinese subsequently sent numerous embassies, around ten every year, to these countries and as far as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucid" title="Seleucid">Seleucid</a> Syria. </p><blockquote><p>Thus more embassies were dispatched to Anxi [Parthia], Yancai [who later joined the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> ], Lijian [Syria under the Greek Seleucids], Tiaozhi (Mesopotamia), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenjiku" title="Tenjiku">Tianzhu</a> [northwestern India]... As a rule, rather more than ten such missions went forward in the course of a year, and at the least five or six. (<i>Hou Hanshu</i>, Later Han History). </p></blockquote><p>These connections marked the beginning of the Silk Road trade network that extended to the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebrey_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebrey-46">[46]</a></sup>
The Chinese campaigned in Central Asia on several occasions, and direct encounters between Han troops and Roman legionaries (probably captured or recruited as mercenaries by the Xiong Nu) are recorded, particularly in the 36 BCE battle of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a> (Joseph Needham, Sidney Shapiro). It has been suggested that the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbow</a> was transmitted to the Roman world on such occasions, although the Greek <a href="/wiki/Gastraphetes" title="Gastraphetes">gastraphetes</a> provides an alternative origin. R. Ernest Dupuy and Trevor N. Dupuy suggest that in 36 BCE,</p><blockquote><p>[A] Han expedition into Central Asia, west of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jaxartes_river" title="Jaxartes river">Jaxartes River</a>, apparently encountered and defeated a contingent of Roman legionaries. The Romans may have been part of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Antony" title="Mark Antony">Antony</a>'s army invading <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>. Sogdiana (modern <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>), east of the Oxus River, on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytimetus" title="Polytimetus">Polytimetus</a> River, was apparently the most easterly penetration ever made by Roman forces in Asia. The margin of Chinese victory appears to have been their crossbows, whose bolts and darts seem easily to have penetrated Roman shields and armour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> </p></blockquote><p>The Roman historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florus" title="Florus">Florus</a> also describes the visit of numerous envoys, which included <i><a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Seres</a></i> (China), to the first Roman Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caesar_Augustus" title="Caesar Augustus">Augustus</a>, who reigned between 27 BCE and 14 CE:
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Even the rest of the nations of the world which were not subject to the imperial sway were sensible of its grandeur, and looked with reverence to the Roman people, the great conqueror of nations. Thus even <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sarmatians" title="Sarmatians">Sarmatians</a> sent envoys to seek the friendship of Rome. Nay, the <a href="/wiki/Serica" title="Serica">Seres</a> came likewise, and the <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">Indians</a> who dwelt beneath the vertical sun, bringing presents of precious stones and pearls and elephants, but thinking all of less moment than the vastness of the journey which they had undertaken, and which they said had occupied four years. In truth, it needed but to look at their complexion to see that they were people of another world than ours.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="/wiki/Henry_Yule" title="Henry Yule">Henry Yule</a>, <i>Cathay and the Way Thither</i> (1866)</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> army regularly policed the trade route against nomadic bandit forces generally identified as <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>. Han general <a href="/wiki/Ban_Chao" title="Ban Chao">Ban Chao</a> led an army of 70,000 <a href="/wiki/Mounted_infantry" title="Mounted infantry">mounted infantry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Light_cavalry" title="Light cavalry">light cavalry</a> troops in the 1st century CE to secure the <a href="/wiki/Trade" title="Trade">trade</a> routes, reaching far west to the Tarim Basin. Ban Chao expanded his conquests across the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pamirs" title="Pamirs">Pamirs</a> to the shores of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a> and the borders of <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> It was from here that the Han general dispatched envoy <a href="/wiki/Gan_Ying" title="Gan Ying">Gan Ying</a> to <a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a> (Rome).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Silk Road essentially came into being from the 1st century BCE, following these efforts by China to consolidate a road to the Western world and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, both through direct settlements in the area of the Tarim Basin and diplomatic relations with the countries of the Dayuan, Parthians and Bactrians further west. The Silk Roads were a "complex network of trade routes" that gave people the chance to exchange goods and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bentley1993p32_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bentley1993p32-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Expansion.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/240px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/360px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Buddhist_Expansion.svg/480px-Buddhist_Expansion.svg.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddhist_Expansion.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">Silk Road transmission of Buddhism</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayana_Buddhism" title="Mahayana Buddhism">Mahayana Buddhism</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Buddhism#History" title="Chinese Buddhism">first entered</a> the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_Empire" title="Chinese Empire">Chinese Empire</a> (<a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty#Religion,_cosmology,_and_metaphysics" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>) during the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Era</a>. The overland and maritime "Silk Roads" were interlinked and complementary, forming what scholars have called the "great circle of Buddhism".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>A maritime Silk Route opened up between Chinese-controlled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giao_Ch%E1%BB%89" title="Giao Chỉ">Giao Chỉ</a> (centred in modern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a>), probably by the 1st century. It extended, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">via ports on the coasts of India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, all the way to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman</a>-controlled ports in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Egypt" title="Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nabataean" title="Nabataean">Nabataean</a> territories on the northeastern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. The earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_glassware" title="Roman glassware">Roman glassware</a> bowl found in China was unearthed from a Western Han tomb in <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a>, dated to the early 1st century BCE, indicating that Roman commercial items were being imported through the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-an_2002_p83_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-an_2002_p83-51">[51]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Chinese dynastic histories</a>, it is from <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhou_(region)" title="Jiaozhou (region)">this region</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations#First_Roman_embassy" title="Sino-Roman relations">Roman embassies</a> arrived in China, beginning in 166 CE during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Marcus_Aurelius" title="Marcus Aurelius">Marcus Aurelius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Huan_of_Han" title="Emperor Huan of Han">Emperor Huan of Han</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Other Roman glasswares have been found in Eastern-Han-era tombs (25–220 CE) more further inland in <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>P.O. Harper asserts that a 2nd or 3rd-century Roman gilt silver plate found in <a href="/wiki/Jingyuan_County,_Gansu" title="Jingyuan County, Gansu">Jingyuan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a>, China with a central image of the Greco-Roman god <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a> resting on a feline creature, most likely came via <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> (i.e. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harper_2002_pp106-107_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-harper_2002_pp106-107-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Valerie_Hansen" title="Valerie Hansen">Valerie Hansen</a> (2012) believed that <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">earliest Roman coins</a> found in China date to the 4th century, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Dominate" title="Dominate">Dominate</a> period, and come from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-valerie_2012_pp97-98_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-valerie_2012_pp97-98-57">[57]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a> (2016) highlights the recent discovery of sixteen <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Principate</a>-era Roman coins found in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">two Han capitals</a>) that were minted during the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperors</a> spanning from <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> (i.e. 1st to 3rd centuries CE).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Helen Wang points out that although these coins were found in China, they were deposited there in the twentieth century, not in ancient times, and therefore do not shed light on historic contacts between China and Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang_2004_p34_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-wang_2004_p34-59">[59]</a></sup> Roman golden <a href="/wiki/Medal" title="Medal">medallions</a> made during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Pius" title="Antoninus Pius">Antoninus Pius</a> and quite possibly his successor Marcus Aurelius have been found at <a href="/wiki/%C3%93c_Eo" title="Óc Eo">Óc Eo</a> in southern <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, which was then part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Funan" title="Kingdom of Funan">Kingdom of Funan</a> bordering the Chinese province of <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> in northern Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-61">[61]</a></sup> Given the archaeological finds of Mediterranean artefacts made by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Malleret" title="Louis Malleret">Louis Malleret</a> in the 1940s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-osborne_2006_pp24-25_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-osborne_2006_pp24-25-61">[61]</a></sup> Óc Eo may have been the same site as the port city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kattigara" title="Kattigara">Kattigara</a> described by <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Geography_(Ptolemy)" title="Geography (Ptolemy)">Geography</a></i> (c. 150 CE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-young_2001_p_29_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-young_2001_p_29-60">[60]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_von_Richthofen" title="Ferdinand von Richthofen">Ferdinand von Richthofen</a> had previously believed it was closer to <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution">Evolution</span></h2>
<h3><span id="Roman_Empire_.2830_BCE.E2.80.933rd_century_CE.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_Empire_(30_BCE–3rd_century_CE)">Roman Empire (30 BCE–3rd century CE)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="842" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg/300px-Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg/450px-Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg/600px-Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Seidenstrasse_GMT_Ausschnitt_Zentralasien.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Central Asia during Roman times, with the first Silk Road</div></div></div>
<p>Soon after the Roman conquest of Egypt in 30 BCE, regular communications and trade between China, Southeast Asia, India, the Middle East, Africa, and Europe blossomed on an unprecedented scale. The Roman Empire inherited eastern trade routes that were part of the Silk Road from the earlier Hellenistic powers and the Arabs. With control of these trade routes, citizens of the Roman Empire received new luxuries and greater prosperity for the Empire as a whole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2010p21_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2010p21-63">[63]</a></sup> The Roman-style glassware discovered in the archeological sites of <a href="/wiki/Gyeongju" title="Gyeongju">Gyeongju</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla kingdom</a> (Korea) showed that Roman artifacts were traded as far as the Korean peninsula.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> The Greco-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_trade_with_India" title="Roman trade with India">Roman trade with India</a> started by <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cyzicus" title="Eudoxus of Cyzicus">Eudoxus of Cyzicus</a> in 130 BCE continued to increase, and according to <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> (II.5.12), by the time of <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, up to 120 ships were setting sail every year from <a href="/wiki/Myos_Hormos" title="Myos Hormos">Myos Hormos</a> in Roman Egypt to India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The Roman Empire connected with the Central Asian Silk Road through their ports in Barygaza (known today as Bharuch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>) and Barbaricum (known today as the city of <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>) and continued along the western coast of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> An ancient "travel guide" to this Indian Ocean trade route was the Greek <a href="/wiki/Periplus_of_the_Erythraean_Sea" title="Periplus of the Erythraean Sea">Periplus of the Erythraean Sea</a> written in 60 CE.
</p><p>The travelling party of <a href="/wiki/Maes_Titianus" title="Maes Titianus">Maës Titianus</a> penetrated farthest east along the Silk Road from the Mediterranean world, probably with the aim of regularising contacts and reducing the role of middlemen, during one of the lulls in Rome's intermittent wars with Parthia, which repeatedly obstructed movement along the Silk Road. Intercontinental trade and communication became regular, organised, and protected by the "Great Powers". Intense <a href="/wiki/Roman_commerce" title="Roman commerce">trade with the Roman Empire</a> soon followed, confirmed by the Roman craze for Chinese silk (supplied through the Parthians), even though the Romans thought silk was obtained from trees. This belief was affirmed by <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca the Younger</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Phaedra_(Seneca)" title="Phaedra (Seneca)">Phaedra</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> in his <a href="/wiki/Georgics" title="Georgics">Georgics</a>. Notably, <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> knew better. Speaking of the <i>bombyx</i> or silk moth, he wrote in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_Histories" title="Natural Histories">Natural Histories</a> "They weave webs, like spiders, that become a luxurious clothing material for women, called silk."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The Romans traded spices, glassware, perfumes, and silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2010p21_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2010p21-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg/220px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg/330px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg/440px-Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cernuschi_Museum_20060812_150.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Westerner on a camel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Wei_dynasty" title="Northern Wei dynasty">Northern Wei dynasty</a> (386–534)</div></div></div>
<p>Roman artisans began to replace yarn with valuable plain silk cloths from China and the <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla Kingdom</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gyeongju" title="Gyeongju">Gyeongju</a>, Korea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-8">[8]</a></sup> Chinese wealth grew as they delivered silk and other luxury goods to the Roman Empire, whose wealthy women admired their beauty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> The Roman Senate issued, in vain, several edicts to prohibit the wearing of silk, on economic and moral grounds: the import of Chinese silk caused a huge outflow of gold, and silk clothes were considered decadent and immoral.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I can see clothes of silk, if materials that do not hide the body, nor even one's decency, can be called clothes.... Wretched flocks of maids labour so that the adulteress may be visible through her thin dress, so that her husband has no more acquaintance than any outsider or foreigner with his wife's body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, and its demand for sophisticated Asian products, <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">collapsed in the fifth century</a>.
</p><p>The unification of Central Asia and Northern India within the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> between the first and third centuries reinforced the role of the powerful merchants from Bactria and <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-72">[72]</a></sup> They fostered multi-cultural interaction as indicated by their 2nd century treasure hoards filled with products from the Greco-Roman world, China, and India, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Bagram" title="Bagram">archeological site of Begram</a>.
</p>
<h3><span id="Byzantine_Empire_.286th.E2.80.9314th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Byzantine_Empire_(6th–14th_centuries)">Byzantine Empire (6th–14th centuries)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_Alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol Alliance">Byzantine-Mongol Alliance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:290px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="9439" data-file-width="19000" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png/288px-Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png/432px-Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png/576px-Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png 2x" width="288"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Major_powers_in_Eurasia_around_555AD.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing Byzantium along with the other major silk road powers during China's <a href="/wiki/Northern_and_Southern_dynasties" title="Northern and Southern dynasties">Southern dynasties</a> period of fragmentation.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Procopius" title="Procopius">Procopius</a> stated that two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christianity" title="Nestorian Christianity">Nestorian Christian</a> monks eventually uncovered the way silk was made. From this revelation, monks were sent by the Byzantine Emperor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a> (ruled 527–565) as spies on the Silk Road from <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> to China and back to <a href="/wiki/Smuggling_of_silkworm_eggs_into_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire">steal the silkworm eggs</a>, resulting in silk production in the Mediterranean, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Soufli#Silk_museums_of_Soufli" title="Soufli">Thrace</a> in northern Greece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius.org_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius.org-73">[73]</a></sup> and giving the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine Empire a monopoly on silk production</a> in medieval Europe. In 568 the Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a> was greeted by a <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> embassy representing <a href="/wiki/Ist%C3%A4mi" title="Istämi">Istämi</a>, ruler of the <a href="/wiki/First_Turkic_Khaganate" title="First Turkic Khaganate">First Turkic Khaganate</a>, who formed an alliance with the Byzantines against <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_I" title="Khosrow I">Khosrow I</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> that allowed the Byzantines to bypass the Sasanian merchants and trade directly with the Sogdians for purchasing Chinese silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Although the Byzantines had already procured silkworm eggs from China by this point, the quality of Chinese silk was still far greater than anything produced in the West, a fact that is perhaps emphasized by the discovery of coins minted by Justin II found in a Chinese tomb of <a href="/wiki/Shanxi" title="Shanxi">Shanxi</a> province dated to the <a href="/wiki/Sui_dynasty" title="Sui dynasty">Sui dynasty</a> (581–618).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solidus_Constans_II_(obverse).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="385" data-file-width="395" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg/170px-Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg/255px-Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg/340px-Solidus_Constans_II_%28obverse%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solidus_Constans_II_(obverse).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)">Coin</a> of <a href="/wiki/Constans_II" title="Constans II">Constans II</a> (r. 641–648), who is named in <a href="/wiki/Twenty-Four_Histories" title="Twenty-Four Histories">Chinese sources</a> as the first of several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_emperor" title="Byzantine emperor">Byzantine emperors</a> to send embassies to the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Both the <i><a href="/wiki/Old_Book_of_Tang" title="Old Book of Tang">Old Book of Tang</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/New_Book_of_Tang" title="New Book of Tang">New Book of Tang</a></i>, covering the history of the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907), record that a new state called <i>Fu-lin</i> (拂菻; i.e. Byzantine Empire) was virtually identical to the previous <i><a href="/wiki/Daqin" title="Daqin">Daqin</a></i> (大秦; i.e. Roman Empire).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup> Several <i>Fu-lin</i> embassies were recorded for the Tang period, starting in 643 with an alleged embassy by <a href="/wiki/Constans_II" title="Constans II">Constans II</a> (transliterated as <i>Bo duo li</i>, 波多力, from his nickname "Kōnstantinos Pogonatos") to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong of Tang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup> The <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Song_(Yuan_dynasty)" title="History of Song (Yuan dynasty)">History of Song</a></i> describes the final embassy and its arrival in 1081, apparently sent by <a href="/wiki/Michael_VII_Doukas" title="Michael VII Doukas">Michael VII Doukas</a> (transliterated as <i>Mie li yi ling kai sa</i>, 滅力伊靈改撒, from <a href="/wiki/Caesar_(title)" title="Caesar (title)">his name and title</a> Michael VII Parapinakēs Caesar) to the court of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Shenzong_of_Song" title="Emperor Shenzong of Song">Emperor Shenzong</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> (960–1279).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, the <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">History of Yuan</a></i> claims that a Byzantine man became a leading astronomer and physician in <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a>, at the court of <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, Mongol founder of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1271–1368) and was even granted <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">the noble title</a> 'Prince of Fu lin' (<a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: 拂菻王; Fú lǐn wáng).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghur</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian" title="Nestorian">Nestorian</a> Christian diplomat <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a>, who set out from his Chinese home in Khanbaliq (Beijing) and acted as a representative for <a href="/wiki/Arghun" title="Arghun">Arghun</a> (a grandnephew of Kublai Khan),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_britannica_raban_bar_sauma_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia_britannica_raban_bar_sauma-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> traveled throughout Europe and attempted to <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">secure military alliances</a> with <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward I of England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_IV" title="Pope Nicholas IV">Pope Nicholas IV</a>, as well as the Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Andronikos_II_Palaiologos" title="Andronikos II Palaiologos">Andronikos II Palaiologos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_britannica_raban_bar_sauma_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia_britannica_raban_bar_sauma-81">[81]</a></sup> Andronikos II had two half-sisters who were married to great-grandsons of <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a>, which made him an in-law with the Yuan-dynasty Mongol ruler in Beijing, Kublai Khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/History_of_Ming" title="History of Ming">History of Ming</a></i> preserves an account where the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>, after founding the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> (1368–1644), had a supposed Byzantine merchant named Nieh-ku-lun (捏古倫) deliver his proclamation about the establishment of a new dynasty to the Byzantine court of <a href="/wiki/John_V_Palaiologos" title="John V Palaiologos">John V Palaiologos</a> in September 1371.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hirth" title="Friedrich Hirth">Friedrich Hirth</a> (1885), <a href="/wiki/Emil_Bretschneider" title="Emil Bretschneider">Emil Bretschneider</a> (1888), and more recently Edward Luttwak (2009) presumed that this was none other than Nicolaus de Bentra, a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Beijing" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Beijing">Roman Catholic bishop of Khanbilaq</a> chosen by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a> to replace the previous archbishop <a href="/wiki/John_of_Montecorvino" title="John of Montecorvino">John of Montecorvino</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bretschneider1871_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bretschneider1871-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-halsall_2000_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-halsall_2000-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Tang_dynasty_.287th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Tang_dynasty_(7th_century)">Tang dynasty (7th century)</span></h3>
