<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaigns_against_the_Western_Turks" title="Tang campaigns against the Western Turks">Tang campaigns against the Western Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_the_Western_Turks" title="Conquest of the Western Turks">Conquest of the Western Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_the_Eastern_Turks" title="Tang campaign against the Eastern Turks">Tang campaign against the Eastern Turks</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty#Trade_and_spread_of_culture" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty § Trade and spread of culture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1199" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/170px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/255px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/340px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Sancai" title="Sancai">sancai</a></i> statue of a <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> man with a <a href="/wiki/Bota_bag" title="Bota bag">wineskin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618–907)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="1283" decoding="async" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg/250px-KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg/375px-KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg/500px-KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KingEndybisEthiopia227-235CE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The empires and city-states of the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axumite_Empire" title="Axumite Empire">Axumites</a> were important trading partners in the ancient Silk Road.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tang_China_669AD.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="472" data-file-width="535" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tang_China_669AD.jpg/220px-Tang_China_669AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tang_China_669AD.jpg/330px-Tang_China_669AD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Tang_China_669AD.jpg/440px-Tang_China_669AD.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tang_China_669AD.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>After the Tang defeated the Gokturks, they reopened the Silk Road to the west.</div></div></div>
<p>Although the Silk Road was initially formulated during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu of Han</a> (141–87 BCE), it was reopened by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Empire" title="Tang Empire">Tang Empire</a> in 639 when <a href="/wiki/Hou_Junji" title="Hou Junji">Hou Junji</a> conquered the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a>, and remained open for almost four decades. It was closed after the Tibetans captured it in 678, but in 699, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empress_Wu" title="Empress Wu">Empress Wu</a>'s period, the Silk Road reopened when the Tang reconquered the <a href="/wiki/Four_Garrisons_of_Anxi" title="Four Garrisons of Anxi">Four Garrisons of Anxi</a> originally installed in 640,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> once again connecting China directly to the West for land-based trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The Tang captured the vital route through the <a href="/wiki/Gilgit" title="Gilgit">Gilgit Valley</a> from Tibet in 722, lost it to the Tibetans in 737, and regained it under the command of the Goguryeo-Korean General <a href="/wiki/Gao_Xianzhi" title="Gao Xianzhi">Gao Xianzhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>While the Turks were settled in the Ordos region (former territory of the <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a>), the Tang government took on the military policy of dominating the central steppe. The Tang dynasty (along with Turkic allies) conquered and subdued Central Asia during the 640s and 650s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> During Emperor Taizong's reign alone, large campaigns were launched against not only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrk" title="Göktürk">Göktürks</a>, but also separate campaigns against the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong%27s_campaign_against_Tuyuhun" title="Emperor Taizong's campaign against Tuyuhun">Tuyuhun</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_the_oasis_states" title="Tang campaign against the oasis states">oasis states</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong%27s_campaign_against_Xueyantuo" title="Emperor Taizong's campaign against Xueyantuo">Xueyantuo</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Taizong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Taizong of Tang">Emperor Taizong</a>, Tang general <a href="/wiki/Li_Jing_(Tang_dynasty)" title="Li Jing (Tang dynasty)">Li Jing</a> <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_the_Eastern_Turks" title="Tang campaign against the Eastern Turks">conquered the Eastern Turkic Khaganate</a>. Under <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Gaozong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Gaozong of Tang">Emperor Gaozong</a>, Tang general <a href="/wiki/Su_Dingfang" title="Su Dingfang">Su Dingfang</a> <a href="/wiki/Conquest_of_the_Western_Turks" title="Conquest of the Western Turks">conquered the Western Turkic Khaganate</a>, an important ally of the Byzantine empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> After these conquests, the Tang dynasty fully controlled the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Xiyu</a>, which was the strategic location astride the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> This led the Tang dynasty to reopen the Silk Road, with this portion named the <b>Tang-Tubo Road</b> ("Tang-Tibet Road") in many historical texts.
</p><p>The Tang dynasty established a second <a href="/wiki/Pax_Sinica" title="Pax Sinica">Pax Sinica</a>, and the Silk Road reached its golden age, whereby Persian and Sogdian merchants benefited from the commerce between East and West. At the same time, the Chinese empire welcomed foreign cultures, making it very cosmopolitan in its urban centres. In addition to the land route, the Tang dynasty also developed the maritime Silk Route. Chinese envoys had been sailing through the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kanchipuram" title="Kanchipuram">India</a> since perhaps the 2nd century BCE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> yet it was during the Tang dynasty that a strong Chinese maritime presence could be found in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (sailing up the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates River</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabia" title="Arabia">Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksum" title="Aksum">Aksum</a> (Ethiopia), and <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Sogdian.E2.80.93T.C3.BCrkic_tribes_.284th.E2.80.938th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sogdian–Türkic_tribes_(4th–8th_centuries)">Sogdian–Türkic tribes (4th–8th centuries)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1144" data-file-width="1356" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg/220px-Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg/330px-Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg/440px-Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caravane_sur_la_Route_de_la_soie_-_Atlas_catalan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>'s caravan on the Silk Road, 1380</div></div></div>
<p>The Silk Road represents an early phenomenon of political and cultural integration due to inter-regional trade. In its heyday, it sustained an international culture that strung together groups as diverse as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magyars" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a>, and Chinese. The Silk Road reached its peak in the west during the time of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>; in the Nile-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus" title="Oxus">Oxus</a> section, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanid Empire</a> period to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Il_Khanate" title="Il Khanate">Il Khanate</a> period; and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinitic" title="Sinitic">sinitic</a> zone from the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period to the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> period. Trade between East and West also developed across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, between Alexandria in Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Guangzhou" title="Guangzhou">Guangzhou</a> in China. Persian Sassanid coins emerged as a means of currency, just as valuable as silk yarn and textiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under its strong integrating dynamics on the one hand and the impacts of change it transmitted on the other, tribal societies previously living in isolation along the Silk Road, and pastoralists who were of barbarian cultural development, were drawn to the riches and opportunities of the civilisations connected by the routes, taking on the trades of marauders or mercenaries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> "Many barbarian tribes became skilled warriors able to conquer rich cities and fertile lands and to forge strong military empires."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radhanites2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="601" data-file-width="1201" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Radhanites2.png/250px-Radhanites2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Radhanites2.png/375px-Radhanites2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Radhanites2.png/500px-Radhanites2.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radhanites2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Eurasia and Africa showing trade networks, c. 870</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdians</a> dominated the east–west trade after the 4th century up to the 8th century, with <a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taraz" title="Taraz">Talas</a> ranking among their main centres in the north. They were the main caravan merchants of Central Asia. Their commercial interests were protected by the resurgent military power of the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrks" title="Göktürks">Göktürks</a>, whose empire has been described as "the joint enterprise of the <a href="/wiki/Ashina_tribe" title="Ashina tribe">Ashina</a> clan and the Soghdians".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> A.V. Dybo noted that "according to historians, the main driving force of the Great Silk Road were not just Sogdians, but the carriers of a mixed Sogdian-Türkic culture that often came from mixed families."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Silk Road gave rise to the clusters of military states of nomadic origins in North China, ushered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Church" title="Nestorian Church">Nestorian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a>, and later <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> religions into Central Asia and China.
</p>
<h3><span id="Islamic_era_.288th.E2.80.9313th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Islamic_era_(8th–13th_centuries)">Islamic era (8th–13th centuries)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Islamic_economics" title="History of Islamic economics">History of Islamic economics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1445" data-file-width="1805" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/220px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/330px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png/440px-Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Baghdad_150_to_300_AH.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Round_city_of_Baghdad" title="Round city of Baghdad">Round city of Baghdad</a> between 767 and 912 was the most important urban node along the Silk Road.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lions,_soie_polychrome_sogdienne,_Asie_centrale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3184" data-file-width="4257" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/220px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/330px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/440px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lions,_soie_polychrome_sogdienne,_Asie_centrale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A lion <a href="/wiki/Motif_(textile_arts)" title="Motif (textile arts)">motif</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> <a href="/wiki/Polychrome" title="Polychrome">polychrome</a> silk, 8th century, most likely from <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">Umayyad</a> era, <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a> had overtaken <a href="/wiki/Ctesiphon" title="Ctesiphon">Ctesiphon</a> as a major trade center until the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_dynasty" title="Abbasid dynasty">Abbasid dynasty</a> built the city of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, which became the most important <a href="/wiki/Cities_along_the_Silk_Road" title="Cities along the Silk Road">city along the silk road</a>.
</p><p>At the end of its glory, the routes brought about the largest continental empire ever, the Mongol Empire, with its political centres strung along the Silk Road (<a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>) in North China, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karakorum_(palace)" title="Karakorum (palace)">Karakorum</a> in central Mongolia, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarmakhand" title="Sarmakhand">Sarmakhand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a> in Northern Iran, realising the political unification of zones previously loosely and intermittently connected by material and cultural goods.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">expanded into Central Asia</a> during the 8th century, under the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a>, while its successor the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a> put a halt to <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_West" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the West">Chinese westward expansion</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> in 751 (near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talas_River" title="Talas River">Talas River</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-100">[100]</a></sup> However, following the disastrous <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> (755–763) and the conquest of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a>, the Tang Empire was unable to reassert its control over Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Contemporary Tang authors noted how the dynasty had gone into decline after this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In 848 the Tang Chinese, led by the commander <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Yichao" title="Zhang Yichao">Zhang Yichao</a>, were only able <a href="/wiki/Guiyi_Circuit" title="Guiyi Circuit">to reclaim</a> the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> from the Tibetans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> The Persian <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid Empire</a> (819–999) centered in Bukhara (<a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>) continued the trade legacy of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdians" title="Sogdians">Sogdians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-100">[100]</a></sup> The disruptions of trade were curtailed in that part of the world by the end of the 10th century and conquests of Central Asia by the Turkic Islamic <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanid Khanate</a>, yet <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christianity" title="Nestorian Christianity">Nestorian Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Central_Asia" title="Buddhism in Central Asia">Buddhism in Central Asia</a> virtually disappeared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the early 13th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_Khwarezmia" title="Mongol conquest of Khwarezmia">Khwarezmia was invaded</a> by the Mongol Empire. The Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> had the once vibrant cities of Bukhara and <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> burned to the ground after besieging them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> However, in 1370 Samarkand saw a revival as the capital of the new <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>. The Turko-Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> forcefully moved artisans and intellectuals from across Asia to Samarkand, making it one of the most important trade centers and cultural <i><a href="/wiki/Entrep%C3%B4t" title="Entrepôt">entrepôts</a></i> of the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Mongol_empire_.2813th.E2.80.9314th_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mongol_empire_(13th–14th_centuries)">Mongol empire (13th–14th centuries)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fonthill_Vase" title="Fonthill Vase">Fonthill Vase</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6607" data-file-width="4409" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg/180px-Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg/270px-Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg/360px-Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_celadon_vase_Branly_71.1886.89.1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan Dynasty</a> era <a href="/wiki/Celadon" title="Celadon">Celadon</a> vase from <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="550" decoding="async" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg/220px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg/330px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg/440px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>'s travels in 1271–1295</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions" title="Mongol invasions">Mongol expansion</a> throughout the Asian continent from around 1207 to 1360 helped bring political stability and re-established the Silk Road (via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Karakorum_(palace)" title="Karakorum (palace)">Karakorum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a>). It also brought an end to the dominance of the Islamic Caliphate over world trade. Because the Mongols came to control the trade routes, trade circulated throughout the region, though they never abandoned their nomadic lifestyle.
</p><p>The Mongol rulers wanted to establish their capital on the Central Asian steppe, so to accomplish this goal, after every conquest they enlisted local people (traders, scholars, artisans) to help them construct and manage their empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The Mongols developed overland and maritime routes throughout the Eurasian continent, Black Sea and the Mediterranean in the west, and the Indian Ocean in the south. In the second half of the thirteenth century Mongol-sponsored business partnerships flourished in the Indian Ocean connecting Mongol Middle East and Mongol China<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mongol diplomat <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a> visited the courts of Europe in 1287–88 and provided a detailed written report to the Mongols. Around the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> became <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">one of the first Europeans</a> to travel the Silk Road to China. His tales, documented in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">The Travels of Marco Polo</a></i>, opened Western eyes to some of the customs of the <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">Far East</a>. He was not the first to bring back stories, but he was one of the most widely read. He had been preceded by numerous Christian missionaries to the East, such as <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benedykt_Polak" title="Benedykt Polak">Benedykt Polak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">Giovanni da Pian del Carpine</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_of_Longjumeau" title="Andrew of Longjumeau">Andrew of Longjumeau</a>. Later envoys included <a href="/wiki/Odoric_of_Pordenone" title="Odoric of Pordenone">Odoric of Pordenone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_de%27_Marignolli" title="Giovanni de' Marignolli">Giovanni de' Marignolli</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_of_Montecorvino" title="John of Montecorvino">John of Montecorvino</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Conti" title="Niccolò de' Conti">Niccolò de' Conti</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">Ibn Battuta</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Moroccan</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> traveller who passed through the present-day Middle East and across the Silk Road from <a href="/wiki/Tabriz" title="Tabriz">Tabriz</a> between 1325 and 1354.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 13th century, efforts were made at forming a <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">Franco-Mongol alliance</a>, with an exchange of ambassadors and (failed) attempts at military collaboration in the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Land" title="Holy Land">Holy Land</a> during the later <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a>. Eventually, the Mongols in the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, after they had destroyed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid" title="Abbasid">Abbasid</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ayyubid" title="Ayyubid">Ayyubid</a> dynasties, converted to Islam and signed the 1323 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aleppo" title="Treaty of Aleppo">Treaty of Aleppo</a> with the surviving Muslim power, the Egyptian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mamluk_Sultanate_(Cairo)" title="Mamluk Sultanate (Cairo)">Mamluks</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some studies indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, which devastated Europe starting in the late 1340s, may have reached Europe from Central Asia (or China) along the trade routes of the Mongol Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> One theory holds that Genoese traders coming from the entrepot of <a href="/wiki/Trabzon" title="Trabzon">Trebizond</a> in northern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> carried the disease to Western Europe; like many other outbreaks of plague, there is strong evidence that it originated in marmots in Central Asia and was carried westwards to the Black Sea by Silk Road traders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Decline_and_disintegration_.2815th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_and_disintegration_(15th_century)">Decline and disintegration (15th century)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Silk_Road&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zheng_He.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="619" data-file-width="977" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zheng_He.png/300px-Zheng_He.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zheng_He.png/450px-Zheng_He.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Zheng_He.png/600px-Zheng_He.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zheng_He.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Port cities on the maritime silk route featured on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">voyages of Zheng He</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The fragmentation of the Mongol Empire loosened the political, cultural, and economic unity of the Silk Road. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkmeni" title="Turkmeni">Turkmeni</a> marching lords seized land around the western part of the Silk Road from the decaying Byzantine Empire. After the fall of the Mongol Empire, the great political powers along the Silk Road became economically and culturally separated. Accompanying the crystallisation of regional states was the decline of nomad power, partly due to the devastation of the Black Death and partly due to the encroachment of sedentary civilisations equipped with <a href="/wiki/Gunpowder" title="Gunpowder">gunpowder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Partial_revival_in_West_Asia">Partial revival in West Asia</span></h3>
<p>Significant is Armenians role in making Europe Asia trade possible by being located in the crossing roads between these two. Armenia had a monopoly on almost all trade roads in this area and a colossal network. From 1700 to 1765, the total export of Persian silk was entirely conducted by Armenians. They were also exporting raisins, coffee beans, figs, Turkish yarn, camel hair, various precious stones, rice, etc., from Turkey and Iran. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Collapse_.2818th_century.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Collapse_(18th_century)">Collapse (18th century)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Silk_Road&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2020</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The silk trade continued to flourish until it was disrupted by the collapse of the Safavid Empire in the 1720s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="New_Silk_Road_.2820th.E2.80.9321st_centuries.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Silk_Road_(20th–21st_centuries)">New Silk Road (20th–21st centuries)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:One-belt-one-road.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="344" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/One-belt-one-road.svg/350px-One-belt-one-road.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/One-belt-one-road.svg/525px-One-belt-one-road.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/One-belt-one-road.svg/700px-One-belt-one-road.svg.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:One-belt-one-road.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plan of the Silk Road with its maritime branch</div></div></div>
<p>In the 20th century, the Silk Road through the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and the overland connections were repeatedly blocked from the First World War on. This also applied to the massive trade barriers of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. It was not until the 1990s that the "old" trade routes began to reactivate again. In addition to the Chinese activities and the integration of Africa, this also applies to the increasing importance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mediterranean_region" title="Mediterranean region">Mediterranean region</a> and the connection to <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> such as the trade center of <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>.
</p><p>Trade along the Silk Road could soon account for almost 40% of total world trade, with a large part taking place by sea. The land route of the Silk Road seems to remain a niche project in terms of transport volume in the future. As a result of the Chinese Silk Road Initiative and investments, trade seems to be intensifying on the relevant routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernhard_Simon_2020_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernhard_Simon_2020-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hellenic_Shipping_News_2018_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hellenic_Shipping_News_2018-118">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maritime_Silk_Road">Maritime Silk Road</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">Maritime Silk Road</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Shanghai,_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone,_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg/220px-Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg/330px-Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg/440px-Port_of_Shanghai%2C_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone%2C_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Port_of_Shanghai,_Yangshan_Deep-water_Harbour_Zone,_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Yangshan Port of <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, China</div></div></div>
<p>The maritime Silk Road follows the old trade route that was opened by the Chinese admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> during the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a>. In particular, the establishment of the lockless <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> then strongly promoted maritime trade between Asia and Europe in this area. While many trade flows were interrupted in the 20th century by the World Wars, the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, from the beginning of the 21st century many of the trading centers that had already existed in the 19th century were activated again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Suez Canal was also continually expanded and its time-saving role in Asia-Europe trade was highlighted. At the beginning of the Maritime Silk Road are the major Chinese ports in <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shenzhen" title="Shenzhen">Shenzhen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Ningbo-Zhoushan" title="Port of Ningbo-Zhoushan">Ningbo-Zhoushan</a>. The Chinese investments in Africa will connect large areas of <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> to the maritime Silk Road and thus to China and directly to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">southern Europe</a> via the Suez Canal. The increasing importance of the Mediterranean as a trading center with its direct, fast connections to <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> is evident from the international investments in port cities of <a href="/wiki/Piraeus" title="Piraeus">Piraeus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a>. Trieste in particular plays a major role in the economic zone in Central Europe known as the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Banana" title="Blue Banana">Blue Banana</a>. This includes a banana-shaped corridor from southern England via the Benelux region, western Germany and Switzerland to northern Italy. The transport via Trieste instead of northern ports such as Rotterdam and Hamburg shortens the delivery time from Shanghai by ten days and from Hong Kong by nine days. On the maritime Silk Road, on which more than half of all containers in the world are already on the move, deep-water ports are being expanded, logistics hubs are being built and new transport routes such as railways and roads in the hinterland are being created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hellenic_Shipping_News_2018_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hellenic_Shipping_News_2018-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilt_2019_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilt_2019-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernhard_Simon_2020_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernhard_Simon_2020-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="2540" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/350px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/525px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg/700px-Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Porto_nuovo_di_Trieste_1.4.2012.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Port of <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a></div></div></div>
<p>Today the maritime silk road runs with its connections from the Chinese coast to the south via <a href="/wiki/Hanoi" title="Hanoi">Hanoi</a> to <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a> through the Strait of Malacca via the Sri Lankan <a href="/wiki/Colombo" title="Colombo">Colombo</a> towards the southern tip of India via <a href="/wiki/Mal%C3%A9" title="Malé">Malé</a>, the capital of the Maldives, to the East African <a href="/wiki/Mombasa" title="Mombasa">Mombasa</a>, from there to <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a>, then through the Red Sea via the Suez Canal to the Mediterranean, there via <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> to the Upper Adriatic region to the northern Italian hub of Trieste with its international free port and its rail connections to <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central Europe</a> and the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a>. As a result, Poland, the Baltic States, Northern Europe and Central Europe are also connected to the maritime silk road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112_116-2"><a href="#cite_note-Marcus_Hernig_2018_pp_112-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilt_2019_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wilt_2019-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Railway_.281990.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Railway_(1990)">Railway (1990)</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trans-Eurasia-Express.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="590" data-file-width="1358" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Trans-Eurasia-Express.png/350px-Trans-Eurasia-Express.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Trans-Eurasia-Express.png/525px-Trans-Eurasia-Express.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Trans-Eurasia-Express.png/700px-Trans-Eurasia-Express.png 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trans-Eurasia-Express.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trans-Eurasia Logistics</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_Land_Bridge" title="Eurasian Land Bridge">Eurasian Land Bridge</a>, a railway through China, Kazakhstan, Mongolia and Russia, is sometimes referred to as the "New Silk Road".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_newroad_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_newroad-130">[130]</a></sup> The last link in one of these two railway routes was completed in 1990, when the railway systems of China and Kazakhstan connected at <a href="/wiki/Alashankou_railway_station" title="Alashankou railway station">Alataw Pass</a> (Alashan Kou). In 2008 the line was used to connect the cities of <a href="/wiki/%C3%9Cr%C3%BCmqi" title="Ürümqi">Ürümqi</a> in China's <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang_Province" title="Xinjiang Province">Xinjiang Province</a> to <a href="/wiki/Almaty" title="Almaty">Almaty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Astana" title="Astana">Astana</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_News_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_News-131">[131]</a></sup> In October 2008 the first <a href="/wiki/Trans-Eurasia_Logistics" title="Trans-Eurasia Logistics">Trans-Eurasia Logistics</a> train reached <a href="/wiki/Hamburg" title="Hamburg">Hamburg</a> from <a href="/wiki/Xiangtan" title="Xiangtan">Xiangtan</a>. Starting in July 2011 the line has been used by a freight service that connects <a href="/wiki/Chongqing" title="Chongqing">Chongqing</a>, China with <a href="/wiki/Duisburg" title="Duisburg">Duisburg</a>, Germany,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shanghaiist.com_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-shanghaiist.com-132">[132]</a></sup> cutting travel time for cargo from about 36 days by container ship to just 13 days by freight train. In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Hewlett-Packard" title="Hewlett-Packard">Hewlett-Packard</a> began moving large freight trains of laptop computers and monitors along this rail route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_newroad_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt_newroad-130">[130]</a></sup> In January 2017, the service sent its first train to London. The network additionally connects to Madrid and Milan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Revival_of_cities_.281966.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Revival_of_cities_(1966)">Revival of cities (1966)</span></h3>
<p>After an earthquake that hit <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a> in Central Asia in 1966, the city had to rebuild itself. Although it took a huge toll on their markets, this commenced a revival of modern silk road cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Belt_and_Road_Initiative_.282013.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Belt_and_Road_Initiative_(2013)">Belt and Road Initiative (2013)</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Belt_and_Road_Initiative" title="Belt and Road Initiative">Belt and Road Initiative</a> and <a href="/wiki/21st_Century_Maritime_Silk_Road" title="21st Century Maritime Silk Road">21st Century Maritime Silk Road</a></div>
<p>During a September 2013 a visit to Kazakhstan, China's Chinese President <a href="/wiki/Xi_Jinping" title="Xi Jinping">Xi Jinping</a> introduced a plan for a New Silk Road from China to Europe. The latest iterations of this plan, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Belt_and_Road_Initiative" title="Belt and Road Initiative">Belt and Road Initiative</a>" (BRI), includes a land-based <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silk_Road_Economic_Belt" title="Silk Road Economic Belt">Silk Road Economic Belt</a> and a <a href="/wiki/21st_Century_Maritime_Silk_Road" title="21st Century Maritime Silk Road">21st Century Maritime Silk Road</a>, with primary points in Ürümqi, <a href="/wiki/Dostyk" title="Dostyk">Dostyk</a>, Nur-Sultan, <a href="/wiki/Gomel" title="Gomel">Gomel</a>, the Belarusian city of <a href="/wiki/Brest,_Belarus" title="Brest, Belarus">Brest</a>, and the Polish cities of <a href="/wiki/Ma%C5%82aszewicze" title="Małaszewicze">Małaszewicze</a> and <a href="/wiki/%C5%81%C3%B3d%C5%BA" title="Łódź">Łódź</a>—which would be <a href="/wiki/Spoke%E2%80%93hub_distribution_paradigm" title="Spoke–hub distribution paradigm">hubs</a> of logistics and transshipment to other countries of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 15 February 2016, with a change in routing, the first train dispatched under the scheme arrived from eastern <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a> Province to Tehran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Though this section does not complete the Silk Road–style overland connection between China and Europe,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">[139]</a></sup> but new railway line connecting China to Europe via Istanbul's has now been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The actual route went through Almaty, <a href="/wiki/Bishkek" title="Bishkek">Bishkek</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dushanbe" title="Dushanbe">Dushanbe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_139-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Routes">Routes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Cities_along_the_Silk_Road" title="Cities along the Silk Road">Cities along the Silk Road</a></div>
<p>The Silk Road consisted of several routes. As it extended westwards from the ancient commercial centres of China, the overland, intercontinental Silk Road divided into northern and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Silk_Road:_Through_Khotan" title="Southern Silk Road: Through Khotan">southern routes</a> bypassing the <a href="/wiki/Taklamakan_Desert" title="Taklamakan Desert">Taklamakan Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lop_Nur" title="Lop Nur">Lop Nur</a>. Merchants along these routes were involved in "relay trade" in which goods changed "hands many times before reaching their final destinations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_route">Northern route</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Northern_Silk_Road" title="Northern Silk Road">Northern Silk Road</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="907" data-file-width="1544" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg/390px-Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg/585px-Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg/780px-Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg.png 2x" width="390"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_Road_in_the_I_century_AD_-_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Silk Road in the 1st century</div></div></div>
<p>The northern route started at <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang'an">Chang'an</a> (now called <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi'an">Xi'an</a>), an ancient capital of China that was moved further east during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han_dynasty" title="Eastern Han dynasty">Later Han</a> to <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>. The route was defined around the 1st century BCE when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Wudi" title="Han Wudi">Han Wudi</a> put an end to harassment by nomadic tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The northern route travelled northwest through the Chinese province of <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> from <a href="/wiki/Shaanxi" title="Shaanxi">Shaanxi</a> Province and split into three further routes, two of them following the mountain ranges to the north and south of the <a href="/wiki/Taklamakan_Desert" title="Taklamakan Desert">Taklamakan Desert</a> to rejoin at <a href="/wiki/Kashgar" title="Kashgar">Kashgar</a>, and the other going north of the <a href="/wiki/Tian_Shan" title="Tian Shan">Tian Shan</a> mountains through <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Talgar" title="Talgar">Talgar</a>, and Almaty (in what is now southeast <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>). The routes split again west of Kashgar, with a southern branch heading down the Alai Valley towards <a href="/wiki/Termez" title="Termez">Termez</a> (in modern Uzbekistan) and <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a> (Afghanistan), while the other travelled through <a href="/wiki/Kokand" title="Kokand">Kokand</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a> (in present-day eastern Uzbekistan) and then west across the <a href="/wiki/Karakum_Desert" title="Karakum Desert">Karakum Desert</a>. Both routes joined the main southern route before reaching ancient <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a>, Turkmenistan. Another branch of the northern route turned northwest past the <a href="/wiki/Aral_Sea" title="Aral Sea">Aral Sea</a> and north of the <a href="/wiki/Caspian_Sea" title="Caspian Sea">Caspian Sea</a>, then and on to the Black Sea.
</p><p>A route for caravans, the northern Silk Road brought to China many goods such as "dates, saffron powder and pistachio nuts from Persia; <a href="/wiki/Frankincense" title="Frankincense">frankincense</a>, aloes and <a href="/wiki/Myrrh" title="Myrrh">myrrh</a> from <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>; sandalwood from India; glass bottles from Egypt, and other expensive and desirable goods from other parts of the world."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> In exchange, the caravans sent back bolts of silk brocade, lacquer-ware, and porcelain.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_route">Southern route</span></h3>
<p>The southern route or Karakoram route was mainly a single route from China through the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram mountains</a>, where it persists in modern times as the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram_Highway" title="Karakoram Highway">Karakoram Highway</a>, a paved road that connects Pakistan and China.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> It then set off westwards, but with southward spurs so travelers could complete the journey by sea from various points. Crossing the high mountains, it passed through northern Pakistan, over the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> mountains, and into Afghanistan, rejoining the northern route near Merv, Turkmenistan. From Merv, it followed a nearly straight line west through mountainous northern Iran, <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, and the northern tip of the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Desert" title="Syrian Desert">Syrian Desert</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> trading ships plied regular routes to <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, while land routes went either north through <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> or south to <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>. Another branch road travelled from <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herat</a> through <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> to <a href="/wiki/Charax_Spasinu" title="Charax Spasinu">Charax Spasinu</a> at the head of the Persian Gulf and across to <a href="/wiki/Petra" title="Petra">Petra</a> and on to <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> and other eastern Mediterranean ports from where ships carried the cargoes to Rome.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southwestern_route">Southwestern route</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tea_Horse_Road" title="Tea Horse Road">Tea Horse Road</a></div>
<p>The southwestern route is believed to be the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmaputra" title="Brahmaputra">Brahmaputra</a> Delta, which has been the subject of international interest for over two millennia. Strabo, the 1st-century Roman writer, mentions the deltaic lands: "Regarding merchants who now sail from Egypt...as far as the Ganges, they are only private citizens..." His comments are interesting as Roman beads and other materials are being found at <a href="/wiki/Wari-Bateshwar_ruins" title="Wari-Bateshwar ruins">Wari-Bateshwar ruins</a>, the ancient city with roots from much earlier, before the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>, presently being slowly excavated beside the Old Brahmaputra in Bangladesh. Ptolemy's map of the <a href="/wiki/Ganges_Delta" title="Ganges Delta">Ganges Delta</a>, a remarkably accurate effort, showed that his informants knew all about the course of the Brahmaputra River, crossing through the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> then bending westward to its source in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>. It is doubtless that this delta was a major international trading center, almost certainly from much earlier than the Common Era. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gemstones" title="Gemstones">Gemstones</a> and other merchandise from <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> were traded in the delta and through it. Chinese archaeological writer Bin Yang and some earlier writers and archaeologists, such as Janice Stargardt, strongly suggest this route of international trade as <a href="/wiki/Sichuan" title="Sichuan">Sichuan</a>–<a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>–<a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> route. According to Bin Yang, especially from the 12th century the route was used to ship bullion from Yunnan (gold and silver are among the minerals in which Yunnan is rich), through northern Burma, into modern Bangladesh, making use of the ancient route, known as the 'Ledo' route. The emerging evidence of the ancient cities of Bangladesh, in particular Wari-Bateshwar ruins, <a href="/wiki/Mahasthangarh" title="Mahasthangarh">Mahasthangarh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhitagarh" title="Bhitagarh">Bhitagarh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bikrampur" title="Bikrampur">Bikrampur</a>, Egarasindhur, and <a href="/wiki/Sonargaon" title="Sonargaon">Sonargaon</a>, are believed to be the international trade centers in this route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maritime_route">Maritime route</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">Maritime Silk Road</a></div>
<p>Maritime Silk Road or Maritime Silk Route refer to the <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/maritime" title="wikt:maritime">maritime</a> section of historic Silk Road that connects China to Southeast Asia, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesian archipelago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, all the way to Egypt and finally Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>The trade route encompassed numbers of bodies of waters; including <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Bengal" title="Gulf of Bengal">Gulf of Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>, and the Red Sea. The maritime route overlaps with historic Southeast Asian maritime trade, <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">Spice trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean_trade" title="Indian Ocean trade">Indian Ocean trade</a> and after 8th century – the Arabian naval trade network. The network also extended eastward to <a href="/wiki/East_China_Sea" title="East China Sea">East China Sea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sea" title="Yellow Sea">Yellow Sea</a> to connect China with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Korean_Peninsula" title="Korean Peninsula">Korean Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_archipelago" title="Japanese archipelago">Japanese archipelago</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_religions">Expansion of religions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="382" data-file-width="286" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg/170px-Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg/255px-Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d7/Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nestorian-Stele-Budge-plate-X.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Stele" title="Nestorian Stele">Nestorian Stele</a>, created in 781, describes the introduction of Nestorian Christianity to China</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Richard_Foltz" title="Richard Foltz">Richard Foltz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinru_Liu" title="Xinru Liu">Xinru Liu</a>, and others have described how trading activities along the Silk Road over many centuries facilitated the transmission not just of goods but also ideas and culture, notably in the area of religions. <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, Buddhism, Christianity, Manichaeism, and Islam all spread across Eurasia through trade networks that were tied to specific religious communities and their institutions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Notably, established Buddhist monasteries along the Silk Road offered a haven, as well as a new religion for foreigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The spread of religions and cultural traditions along the Silk Roads, according to <a href="/wiki/Jerry_H._Bentley" title="Jerry H. Bentley">Jerry H. Bentley</a>, also led to <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a>. One example was the encounter with the Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Xiongnu" title="Xiongnu">Xiongnu</a> nomads. These unlikely events of cross-cultural contact allowed both cultures to adapt to each other as an alternative. The Xiongnu adopted Chinese agricultural techniques, dress style, and lifestyle, while the Chinese adopted Xiongnu military techniques, some dress style, music, and dance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerry_H_1993_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jerry_H_1993-151">[151]</a></sup> Perhaps most surprising of the cultural exchanges between China and the Xiongnu, Chinese soldiers sometimes defected and converted to the Xiongnu way of life, and stayed in the steppes for fear of punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jerry_H_1993_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jerry_H_1993-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Nomadic mobility played a key role in facilitating inter-regional contacts and cultural exchanges along the ancient Silk Roads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hermes_2018_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-hermes_2018-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frachetti_2017_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-frachetti_2017-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_of_Christianity">Transmission of Christianity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East" title="Church of the East">Church of the East</a></div>
<p>The transmission of Christianity was primarily known as Nestorianism on the Silk Road. In 781, an inscribed stele shows Nestorian Christian missionaries arriving on the Silk Road. Christianity had spread both east and west, simultaneously bringing Syriac language and evolving the forms of worship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transmission_of_Buddhism">Transmission of Buddhism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">Silk Road transmission of Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_of_Miran.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1783" data-file-width="1621" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Buddha_of_Miran.png/170px-Buddha_of_Miran.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Buddha_of_Miran.png/255px-Buddha_of_Miran.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Buddha_of_Miran.png/340px-Buddha_of_Miran.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_of_Miran.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fragment of a wall painting depicting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Miran_(Xinjiang)" title="Miran (Xinjiang)">Miran</a> along the Silk Road (200AD - 400AD)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="470" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/170px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/255px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/340px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A blue-eyed <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Central_Asia" title="Buddhism in Central Asia">Central Asian monk</a> teaching an East-Asian monk, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik" title="Bezeklik">Bezeklik</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, China, 9th century; the monk on the right is possibly <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> although more likely <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gasparini_2014_pp134-163_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-gasparini_2014_pp134-163-156">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The transmission of Buddhism to China via the Silk Road began in the 1st century CE, according to a semi-legendary account of an ambassador sent to the West by the Chinese Emperor <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Ming_of_Han" title="Emperor Ming of Han">Ming</a> (58–75). During this period Buddhism began to spread throughout Southeast, East, and Central Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> Mahayana, Theravada, and Tibetan Buddhism are the three primary forms of Buddhism that spread across Asia via the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Buddhist movement was the first large-scale missionary movement in the history of world religions. Chinese missionaries were able to assimilate Buddhism, to an extent, to native Chinese Daoists, which brought the two beliefs together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Buddha's community of followers, the <a href="/wiki/Sangha" title="Sangha">Sangha</a>, consisted of male and female monks and laity. These people moved through India and beyond to spread the ideas of Buddha.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> As the number of members within the Sangha increased, it became costly so that only the larger cities were able to afford having the Buddha and his disciples visit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> It is believed that under the control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushan" title="Kushan">Kushans</a>, Buddhism was spread to China and other parts of Asia from the middle of the first century to the middle of the third century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> Extensive contacts started in the 2nd century, probably as a consequence of the expansion of the Kushan empire into the Chinese territory of the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, due to the missionary efforts of a great number of Buddhist monks to Chinese lands. The first missionaries and translators of Buddhists scriptures into Chinese were either Parthian, Kushan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdian</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuchean" title="Kuchean">Kuchean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="683" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/170px-AsokaKandahar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/255px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/AsokaKandahar.jpg/340px-AsokaKandahar.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AsokaKandahar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bilingual edict (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a>) by Indian Buddhist King <a href="/wiki/Ashoka" title="Ashoka">Ashoka</a>, 3rd century BCE; <i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a>. This edict advocates the adoption of "godliness" using the Greek term <a href="/wiki/Eusebeia" title="Eusebeia">Eusebeia</a> for <a href="/wiki/Dharma" title="Dharma">Dharma</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kabul" title="Kabul">Kabul</a> Museum.</div></div></div>
<p>One result of the spread of Buddhism along the Silk Road was displacement and conflict. The Greek Seleucids were exiled to Iran and Central Asia because of a new Iranian dynasty called the Parthians at the beginning of the 2nd century BCE, and as a result, the Parthians became the new middlemen for trade in a period when the Romans were major customers for silk. Parthian scholars were involved in one of the first-ever Buddhist text translations into the Chinese language. Its main trade centre on the Silk Road, the city of <a href="/wiki/Merv" title="Merv">Merv</a>, in due course and with the coming of age of Buddhism in China, became a major Buddhist centre by the middle of the 2nd century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Knowledge among people on the silk roads also increased when Emperor Ashoka of the Maurya dynasty (268–239 BCE) converted to Buddhism and raised the religion to official status in his northern Indian empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz_1999_38_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz_1999_38-166">[166]</a></sup>
