</p><p>From the 4th century CE onward, Chinese pilgrims also started to travel on the Silk Road to India to get improved access to the original Buddhist scriptures, with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fa-hsien" title="Fa-hsien">Fa-hsien</a>'s pilgrimage to India (395–414), and later <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> (629–644) and <a href="/wiki/Hyecho" title="Hyecho">Hyecho</a>, who traveled from Korea to India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> The travels of the priest Xuanzang were fictionalized in the 16th century in a fantasy adventure novel called <i><a href="/wiki/Journey_to_the_West" title="Journey to the West">Journey to the West</a></i>, which told of trials with demons and the aid given by various disciples on the journey.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon,_from_Sarnath,_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art,_Dahem_Berlin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg/170px-A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg/255px-A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg/340px-A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon%2C_from_Sarnath%2C_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art%2C_Dahem_Berlin.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_statue_depicting_Buddha_giving_sermon,_from_Sarnath,_now_at_Museum_of_Asian_Art,_Dahem_Berlin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue depicting Buddha giving a sermon, from <a href="/wiki/Sarnath" title="Sarnath">Sarnath</a>, 3,000 km (1,864 mi) southwest of Urumqi, Xinjiang, 8th century</div></div></div>
<p>There were many different schools of Buddhism travelling on the Silk Road. The Dharmaguptakas and the Sarvastivadins were two of the major Nikaya schools. These were both eventually displaced by the Mahayana, also known as "Great Vehicle". This movement of Buddhism first gained influence in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz_1999_38_166-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz_1999_38-166">[166]</a></sup> The Mahayana, which was more of a "pan-Buddhist movement" than a school of Buddhism, appears to have begun in northwestern India or Central Asia. It formed during the 1st century BCE and was small at first, and the origins of this "Greater Vehicle" are not fully clear. Some Mahayana scripts were found in northern Pakistan, but the main texts are still believed to have been composed in Central Asia along the Silk Road. These different schools and movements of Buddhism were a result of the diverse and complex influences and beliefs on the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> With the rise of Mahayana Buddhism, the initial direction of Buddhist development changed. This form of Buddhism highlighted, as stated by Xinru Liu, "the elusiveness of physical reality, including material wealth." It also stressed getting rid of material desire to a certain point; this was often difficult for followers to understand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Liu2010p21_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2010p21-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 5th and 6th centuries CE, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merchants" title="Merchants">merchants</a> played a large role in the spread of religion, in particular Buddhism. Merchants found the moral and ethical teachings of Buddhism an appealing alternative to previous religions. As a result, merchants supported Buddhist monasteries along the Silk Road, and in return, the Buddhists gave the merchants somewhere to stay as they traveled from city to city. As a result, merchants spread Buddhism to foreign encounters as they traveled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Merchants also helped to establish <a href="/wiki/Diaspora" title="Diaspora">diaspora</a> within the communities they encountered, and over time their cultures became based on Buddhism. As a result, these communities became centers of literacy and culture with well-organized marketplaces, lodging, and storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The voluntary conversion of Chinese ruling elites helped the spread of Buddhism in East Asia and led Buddhism to become widespread in Chinese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> The Silk Road transmission of Buddhism essentially ended around the 7th century with the rise of Islam in Central Asia.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism_on_the_Silk_Road">Judaism on the Silk Road</span></h3>
<p>Adherents to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_faith" title="Jewish faith">Jewish faith</a> first began to travel eastward from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> conquest of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a> in 559 by the armies of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judean" title="Judean">Judean</a> slaves freed after the Persian conquest of Babylon dispersed throughout the Persian Empire. Some Judeans could have traveled as far east as <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdia</a>, though there is not clear evidence for this early settlement of Judeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup> After settlement, it is likely that most Judeans took up trades in commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup> Trading along the silk trade networks by Judean merchants increased as the trade networks expanded. By the classical age, when trade goods traveled from as far east as China to as far west as <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, Judean merchants in Central Asia would have been in an advantageous position to participate in trade along the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-2"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup> A group of Judean merchants originating from Gaul known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radanites" title="Radanites">Radanites</a> were one group of Judean merchants that had thriving trade networks from China to Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-3"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup> This trade was facilitated by a positive relationship the Radanites were able to foster with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khazar" title="Khazar">Khazar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkic_people" title="Turkic people">Turks</a>. The Khazar Turks served as a good spot in between China and Rome, and the Khazar Turks saw a relationship with the Radanites as a good commercial opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-4"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Richard Foltz "there is more evidence for Iranian influence on the formation of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Jewish</a> [religious] ideas than the reverse." Concepts of a <a href="/wiki/Paradise" title="Paradise">paradise</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">heaven</a>) for the good and a place of suffering (<a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">hell</a>) for the wicked, and a form or world-ending <a href="/wiki/Apocalypse" title="Apocalypse">apocalypse</a> came from <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> religious ideas, and this is supported by a lack of such ideas from pre-exile Judean sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-5"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup> The origin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_devil" title="The devil">the devil</a> is also said to come from the Iranian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angra_Mainyu" title="Angra Mainyu">Angra Mainyu</a>, an evil figure in <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persian mythology</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:42_172-6"><a href="#cite_note-:42-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Expansion_of_the_arts">Expansion of the arts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_art" title="Silk Road transmission of art">Silk Road transmission of art</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WindGods.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="970" decoding="async" height="107" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/WindGods.JPG/350px-WindGods.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/WindGods.JPG/525px-WindGods.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/WindGods.JPG/700px-WindGods.JPG 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WindGods.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iconographical evolution of the Wind God. Left: Greek Wind God from <a href="/wiki/Hadda,_Afghanistan" title="Hadda, Afghanistan">Hadda</a>, 2nd century. Middle: Wind God from <a href="/wiki/Kizil_Caves" title="Kizil Caves">Kizil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, 7th century. Right: Japanese Wind God <a href="/wiki/F%C5%ABjin" title="Fūjin">Fujin</a>, 17th century.</div></div></div>
<p>Many artistic influences were transmitted via the Silk Road, particularly through Central Asia, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_art" title="Persian art">Iranian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_art" title="Chinese art">Chinese</a> influences could intermix. <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a> represents one of the most vivid examples of this interaction. Silk was also a representation of art, serving as a religious symbol. Most importantly, silk was used as currency for trade along the silk road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>These artistic influences can be seen in the development of Buddhism where, for instance, Buddha was first depicted as human in the Kushan period. Many scholars have attributed this to Greek influence. The mixture of Greek and Indian elements can be found in later Buddhist art in China and throughout countries on the Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>
</p><p>The production of art consisted of many different items that were traded along the Silk Roads from the East to the West. One common product, the <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a>, was a blue stone with golden specks, which was used as paint after it was ground into powder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2>
<p>On 22 June 2014, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Educational,_Scientific_and_Cultural_Organization" title="United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization">United Nations Educational, Scientific and Cultural Organization (UNESCO)</a> named the Silk Road a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silk_Road_UNESCO_World_Heritage_Sites" title="Silk Road UNESCO World Heritage Sites">World Heritage Site</a> at the 2014 Conference on World Heritage. The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/World_Tourism_Organization" title="World Tourism Organization">World Tourism Organization</a> has been working since 1993 to develop sustainable <a href="/wiki/International_tourism" title="International tourism">international tourism</a> along the route with the stated goal of fostering peace and understanding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-silkroad.unwto.org_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-silkroad.unwto.org-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>To commemorate the Silk Road becoming a UNESCO World Heritage Site, the <a href="/wiki/China_National_Silk_Museum" title="China National Silk Museum">China National Silk Museum</a> announced a "Silk Road Week" to take place 19–25 June 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bishkek and Almaty each have a major east–west street named after the Silk Road (<a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_language" title="Kyrgyz language">Kyrgyz</a>: <span lang="ky">Жибек жолу</span>, <i>Jibek Jolu</i> in Bishkek, and <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>: <span lang="kk">Жібек жолы</span>, <i>Jibek Joly</i> in Almaty). There is also a Silk Road in Macclesfield, UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p>

Berlin, Germany: Dietrich Reimer. pp. 496–507.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=China.+Ergebnisse+eigener+Reisen+und+darauf+gegr%C3%BCndeter+Studien&amp;rft.place=Berlin%2C+Germany&amp;rft.pages=496-507&amp;rft.pub=Dietrich+Reimer&amp;rft.date=1877&amp;rft.aulast=Richthofen&amp;rft.aufirst=Ferdinand+von&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbabel.hathitrust.org%2Fcgi%2Fpt%3Fid%3Dnyp.33433090756903%3Bview%3D1up%3Bseq%3D560&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASilk+Road"></span> From p. 496: <i>"Ergänzende Nachrichten über den westlichen Theil einer der früheren Seidenstrassen erhalten wir wiederum durch MARINUS, der hier ganz seinem Berichterstatter, dem Agenten des Macedoniers MAËS (s. oben S. 478), folgt."</i> (On the other hand, we obtain additional information about the western part of one of the earlier Silk Roads via <a href="/wiki/Marinus_of_Tyre" title="Marinus of Tyre">Marinus</a>, who here closely follows his correspondent, the agent of the Macedonian <a href="/wiki/Maes_Titianus" title="Maes Titianus">Maës</a> (see p. 478 above).)</li></ul>
